Neither had a good track record on that score.  But we'll never know if they would've stayed together, lived happily ever after.  Damn.<br /><br />I don't know if the author intentionally inserted red herrings to ultimately surprise, or if he was saying \\"things seem one way, but they're actually another,\\" despite appearances, or both.  For example, there was little reason for him to go into the detail about Sylvie (spelling?) regretting having left Dex, resenting Emma, suggesting that they might reconcile later.  But that doesn't happen.<br /><br />Throughout the book there's this underlying theme, press, that they're meant to be together, the parents and friends think so, approve, seem to expect it, like it was destined to be.  Then it finally happens, but apparently it was NOT destined to be.  Unless the point was ultimately to make Dex become a better person.  Of course, perhaps there was no point, no destiny involved, just mistaken human attributions of such.  The author did a good job too of showing that people adapt to WHATEVER happens.<br /><br />I know it's trite, but I would've found it more satisfying if they'd either lived happily ever after, or better still, found that their longing for each other had been based on quicksand, petered out, and then one or both of them found TRUE love.  I would have liked it if Dex had reconciled with Sylvie, both of them now older and wiser and more appreciative of each other.<br /><br />This book communicated such strong feelings, for example, regret over missed opportunities.  They each kept wanting to call and make contact, kept thinking of each other, but allowed themselves to be distracted by other things, sidetracked by self-doubt in particular.  Then when they'd overcome the doubt and call anyway, they'd just miss the other one.  Of course the reader is privy to the thought processes of both, so we know they are each thinking of the other, but sadly, mostly out of sync.<br /><br />I was affected by the sense of urgency (spanning youth thru middle age), seeming powerlessness over circumstances, pessimism or hopelessness, futility, regret.  Life is full of such moments.  I thought the author captured the shock of aging well.  Was the ending satisfying?  I couldn't help but compare it to the wonderful British TV show as \\"As Time Goes By,\\" which I found more satisfying (sorry- knee-jerk longing for happy endings).<br /><br />Was the book memorable, thought-provoking? YES.  It will stick with me for a long time.  Definitely worth reading.<br /><br />UPDATE: I later saw the film, and while it had quite a few lousy reviews, and was unfavorably compared with the novel, this was one of the few cases in which I actually preferred the movie to the book.  I thought the movie retained the best bits of the book and actually improved upon it.r if he was saying \\"things seem one way, but they're actually another,\\" despite appearances, or both. B005GVW05Q|5.0|Now a sober man with a Hollywood wife, a Hollywood house and his own record label, Pax Am, Ryan Adams has released the first record of his second act, a mellow and warm singer/songwriter effort called Ashes & Fire. The two things you're bound to hear about this record - if you're the investigative type - is that: 1) The record is quite mellow and fells like something of a brother to Neil Young's Harvest and After the Goldrush; and 2) Adams has become, maybe above all else, an incredible vocalist.    In a 2001 interview, following the release of his make-or-break sophomore solo release, Gold, Adams spoke about his then-strict routine, where he forced himself to spend an hour each day working on songwriting, an hour each day practicing guitar and an hour or more each day singing. It was no surprise then that, following Gold, Adams became one of the most prolific - and consistent - songwriters of his generation, releasing 12 albums between his breakthrough release, 1997's Stranger's Almanac, and today's Ashes & Fire. All along it's been said that Adams was a heavy user, living out the mission set forth in his 2001 song \\"Firecracker,\\" where he sand \\"Everyone wants to go forever / I just want to burn up hard and bright.\\" Clearly, things have changed, and Ashes & Fire is the document.    What first took my attention was the look of the record. Adams, also a painter and novelist, had never struck me as a man with an overall aesthetic like, say, Thom Yorke or Jack White. That is, not one to speak too highly of. Mostly, Adams - for me that is - was a lousy painter and poet who could make great, great songs that sometimes had very good lyrics. Then came Ashes, a beautifully presented record that begs me to believe that, as a sober adult, the man has more tolerance of the things outside the recording studio. And while the beautiful presentation of the record does seem distantly symbolic in relation to Our Man's transition from punk to adult, the real star of the show, as it should be, are the 11 songs - a collection Adams himself has already called his \\"best yet.\\"    Opener \\"Dirty Rain,\\" a delicate and detailed composition heaped with 70s-era style turns, certainly starts things off right, working as an introduction to the artist's new era. In the song (or so we assume), Adams narrates from a third person perspective, juxtaposing his early 2000s rock star residency in L.A. with his modern - and much more settled - tenure in the town (he inhabited New York City in the interim). A beautiful opening that will resonate deeply with his longtime fans, putting to rest the inevitable \\"he was better when he was stoned\\" chatter in a classy, admirable manner.    Track two, the record's title track, is the most upbeat offering of the collection, feeling like something of a celebration of the man's abilities. He howls, strums and bangs in a way only he can. You'd be hard pressed to find a working rock musician with the ability to make a song as classic-sounding as \\"Ashes & Fire.\\" Maybe they can sing as good as Adams, or arrange songs just as good. Or they're a strong songwriter, sure. But a master of all three key elements, at once, on one song? Classic tracks like this are rare. Very rare. But far less rare for Adams than most.    If you've heard the above-mentioned Young records and are familiar with Adams' records like Gold, 29, Easy Tiger and Jacksonville City Nights, then you should have a pretty good idea of what Ashes & Fire sounds like. Overall the record is consistently mellow and most definitely thick and warm. There are instant classics like \\"Save Me\\" and \\"Lucky Now\\" and slow burners like \\"Invisible Riverside.\\" The writing isn't his most interesting to date but the singing soars like never before and the arrangements are heartbreakingly beautiful. Sure, I'd like to see the guy stomp his boots a little more here and there (he's one of the best ever at that), and I know he has more adventurous records up his sleeve. But if you're looking forfront-to-back listenability, it's hard to top this collection sweetly twangy, surprisingly uplifting tunes. The biggest difference here is that, for the first time ever, Adams has offered up a collection of songs that feel, for the most part, wholly positive. Hopeful. Tender. He rarely went there before (only \\"Dance All Night\\" and \\"So Alive\\" come to mind at the moment), to that positive place in his songwriting heart, but when he did, it was always damn good. Rest assured, Ashes & Fire, a new era Ryan Adams for sure, continues the streak, but with a new twist. His best since 2007's classic Easy Tiger.    97/100    Read more of my music- and film-related writing at [...]

B005IGVYUG|5.0|What more is there to say about Tom Waits now, 38 years and 17 studio albums after his 1973 debut, Closing Time? Of all the men I know of, the ones who have made me feel okay about the disgusting world, Tom stands tall, up there with complicated heroes like Ernest Hemingway, Charles Bukowski, Jim Jarmusch, Neil Young and Paul Westerberg. All damaged, gritty, wildly unique and creative men who found their way through alleys and backstreets, never taking the bait. Among them Waits, in my mind, maybe stands the tallest. Or maybe, I suppose, I'm just floating high on the buzz of Bad As Me, the man's first collection of brand new material since 2004's Real Gone and one of the best works of his storied career.    There comes a time in most artist's life, probably towards the end of when they expect themselves to be functional, where priorities and goals change. Having a legacy is no longer a joke, but a hard reality to stomach. Suddenly arrives a need to create something perfect - something signature. Maybe they've already done so in the past; doesn't matter, it has to happen again, before it's over. For Bob Dylan, it was 1997's tremendous Time Our of Mind and for Hemingway it was stack after stack of unpublished works, including \\"Islands In the Stream.\\" For Waits, it seems, it's Bad As Me, a bulletproof 13-song record that plays through almost like a best-of collection - a release just begging for that rare tag of \\"late era masterpiece.\\" And yes, Bad As Me is most definitely a masterpiece - the culmination of a career that sees a man looking back at his life, playing nostalgic and even bettering his younger self at times. When \\"Get Lost,\\" one of the record's many highlights, kicks in I can't help but want to race home and put on Down By Law, a cool-as-hell Jim Jarmusch film that starred a prime-era Waits as his most cool, most dangerous self.    Just when you thought all the anthems had been written, Waits offers up \\"Get Lost\\" and, even more notably, the record's title track, \\"Bad As Me.\\" The second \\"Bad\\" ended I, for once, turned to my listening pal and said something true: \\"Broken men will be quoting this song to broken women for decades to come, not for comfort but for relief.\\" And it's true: Waits has that rare, indefinable ability to put his thumb on the little corners of life.  \\"Talking At The Same Time,\\" another new classic, sees Waits exhausted, howling sad verses for a broken generation, summing things up nicely: \\"Everybody knows umbrellas cost more in the rain / And all the news is bad / Is there any other kind? / Everybody's talking at the same time.\\" Political? Yeah. Annoying? No. Timeless - yes! And that's the brilliance of Tom Waits. Oh, and there's this song called \\"Hell Broke Luce,\\" but I won't even go there, for lack of ink.    I could go on. I could go on and on and on. there's so much to love here. Bad As MeI is the document of a master, working at the height of his powers when you least expected him to do so. It's a creative, fully-baked album that just might go down as his most consistent, accessible and even masterful record to date. Who'd have thunk it? Tom Waits, at age 61, released the best record of 2011, and he made it look so easy.    100/100    Check out more of my music- and film-related writing at [...]

1594744769|4.0|In some ways, I'm surprised I liked this as much as I did.  I'm not sure why, maybe it's because I love the title/cover combo, or because I love the idea behind it, or because quirky can sometimes go horribly, horribly wrong; whatever the reason, I was kind of bracing to be let down in this one.  And since it reads a little dispassionately in the beginning, I had a hard time staying engaged and thought my doom-and-gloom expectations were going to be - well, satisfied doens't seem like the right word...<br />But I thought I was going to be disappointed, and I wasn't. (yay!)<br /><br />Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children is intensely creative and unique, consistently giving me things I wasn't expecting.  This is not to say I didn't have issues with the book, because I did.  Though I love the idea of using quirky, odd photos to help narrate the story, there were certainly times when those photos seemed more forced on the story than supplemental to it.  There were times, too, where it just felt so damned over-written. I just wanted to say, 'Pull back a little, buddy.  One metaphor is fine.'  And as I said, it sometimes felt a little dispassionate.  But neither of these was a consistent problem, and the times when the book or the tone was nailed far outweighed the times when they were not.<br /><br />I think this is one of those books that you should know going in whether you are going to like it.  If you know that you like things a little quirky, a little dark, a little macabre and a whole lot strange, then yes, you are going to enjoy this.  If you don't, you probably won't.  The characters the reader is introduced to certainly live up to their moniker of peculiar.  Some are just mildly shocking ala a circus freak show, and some are downright unsettling (I'm talking about you, boy who makes little clay golems, then brings them to life with mouse hearts and makes them fight each other)...  There's a creepy \\"off\\" tone to a lot of what goes on, and the threat of violence and being, you know, hunted down and eaten, so yeah, it's a good Halloween read for sure. ;P<br /><br />What I loved, though, was getting things I didn't expect.  It's rare for a book to surprise me, and this one did so pretty consistently.  There were some great lines and bits of unexpected description that just tickled me and had me pulling out my post-its tabs*.  And there were characters and relationships among them that I did not see coming, and aspects of the villains that I didn't see coming (and I am rarely surprised or pleased by a villain).  Most of all, though, I was surprised by the whole plot and who it all works together. Beyond the expected elements of horror and mystery, there is romance and history and - something I can't get into it without being very spoilery - there was a crucial element to the plot, hinging on an ability of the titular Miss Peregrine, that I just did not see coming.  And I loved it.<br /><br />And I sort of feel like that's all I can say without starting to give some major things away.  As most people know now, there will be a second book (which, though it could be read and work as a stand-alone, I think a 2nd book was a bit of a given), and there will be a movie. And this I am very eager for, as I kept picturing scenes while I was reading.  It's very visual and some elements of the setting I just cannot wait to see onscreen.  There is a sense of wonder that I hope they can catch and even expand on.<br /><br />Oh, and I love the design of the book.  I know it's silly, but it gets points.  Almost everything about the design is just a little tiny bit different than other books, showing that thought went in to nearly every aspect of it, from the very squared-off binding to the end papers and chapter-pages, etc.  It's nice, that level of thought and attention to detail. I approve.<br /><br />*\\"But beyond all that, above the houses and fields and sheep doddering around like puffs of cotton candy, I could see tongues of dense fog licking over the ridge in the distance, where this world ended and the next one began, cold, damp, and sunless.\\"<br />Though a bit overwritten, I just adored the image of the 'sheep doddering around like puffs of cotton candy.'  ;)d and the next one began, cold, damp, and sunless.\\"  Though a bit overwritten, I just adored the image of the 'sheep doddering around like puffs of cotton candy.'  ;)

1609611543|5.0|I just read in Time Magazine this month that menarchy (the age of the onset of menstruation) has been dropping precipitously for girls, and that exposure to this level of hormones has been linked to cancer and other health problems. Why are girls getting their periods so much sooner? Obesity is one guess. Girls are substantially more obese now than they ever have been. The article concludes that some really bad things are happening to our 'Little Women,' but, well, nobody knows quite why.<br /><br />I think Dr. Davis would take a guess at this, that it's another example of wheat doing funny things to our bodies. I also know that I would agree. \\"Healthy whole grains,\\" which comprise such a huge proportion of our modern diets, and which have been so highly genetically manipulated, may be making girls fat and causing massive outputs of insulin that could be changing their biological functions in ways that we have not yet fathomed. Since I read Wheat Belly, I have noticed more and more such extensions and confirmations of Dr. Davis' thesis, namely that inordinate increases in insulin have caused a vast and increasing number of health problems, and that most likely culprit behind the insulin flood is wheat. And wheat, like any convincing villain, is everywhere. All of this is exacerbated by current government-backed dietary recommendations that emphasize the importance of consuming plenty of \\"healthy whole grains.\\"<br /><br />Dr. Davis suggests that today's wheat is not the same wheat that we ate 50 years ago. He cites an interesting study where scientists analyzed blood collected between 1948 and 1952 for routine physical examinations of healthy military recruits. The scientists tested the vintage blood for IgA antibodies. After making sure that IgA antibodies were stable, they tested the blood for evidence of celiac disease. Comparing the preserved blood samples to blood drawn from similar contemporary subjects, investigators found that celiac disease is four times more prevalent in today's subjects than it was in those of a half-century ago. Similar studies found similar results. What has changed? Human DNA has probably not changed that much; we are separated from those men by a maximum of four generations. Wheat, however, may have changed substantially. Wheat and grasses have a very flexible polyploid genetic make-up. Grasses change quickly, and corn has even been shown to have a jumping gene. Wheat may have changed on its own over the last few centuries, but in the last few decades has been even more fundamentally altered by far more radical means, including radiation and chemical mutagenesis - all to increase production values and profits. This new wheat would not be recognizable to our ancestors, Davis asserts. And this new wheat might just be making us fat and sick.<br /><br />My own experience in eliminating wheat has resulted in curing what I thought would be lifelong chronic dyspepsia. I've lost a bit of weight around my middle, which is nice. I did not have much to lose.<br /><br />Written the way a good non-fiction work should be written: the premise is laid out in full, then thoroughly documented. Additional details are added as well, in fact, the scariest thing that Dr. Davis discussed were the advanced glycation end products (AGE) that arise from high insulin levels. AGE products have a high impact on biological functions as disparate as vision, circulation and mental function, and have been associated with cataracts, atherosclerosis and dementia. Dr. Davis' assertions are as global as they are novel, and as well supported with copious peer-reviewed scientific research. Here are a few papers he cites:<br /><br />Foster-Powell, Holt SHA, Brand-Miller JC. International table of glycemic index and glycemic load values: 2002. Am J Clin Nutr. 2002;76(1):5-56.<br /><br />Kl&ouml;ting N, Fasshauer M, Dietrich A, et al. Insulin sensitive obesity. Am J Physiol Endocrinol Metab. 2010 Jun 22.<br /><br />DeMarco VG, Johnson MS,Whaley-Connell AT, Sowers JR. Cytokine abnormalities in the etiology of the cardiometabolic syndrome. Curr Hypertens Rep. 2010 Apr;12(2):93-8.<br /><br />Matsuzawa Y. Establishment of a concept of visceral fat syndrome and discovery of adiponectin. Proc Jpn Acad Ser B Phys Biol Sci. 2010;86(2):131-41.<br /><br />Funahashi T, Matsuzawa Y. Hypoadiponectinemia: a common basis for diseases associated with overnutrition. Curr Atheroscler Rep. 2006 Sep;8(5):433-8.<br /><br />Depr&eacute;s J, Lemieux I, Bergeron J, et al. Abdominal obesity and the metabolic syndrome: contributions to global cardiometabolic risk. B000MV90IU|5.0|It's difficult to imagine a more iconic film than Bullitt. It has always represented, for me, a core theatrical experience, the essence of what it means to see a film. We all waited for many years for Bullitt to come out on Blu-Ray, which is the only format that does this film \\"justice\\". Watching Bullitt in anything less than widescreen format is a waste of time. I am delighted with the Blu-Ray release -- It is everything, technically, I could wish for.<br /><br />And yet it has been so many, MANY years since the initial 1968 release, that one must now recollect what makes this classic crime film so special and unique. What was cutting edge and fresh then, has achieved a cliche/cult status today.<br /><br />There are a lot of 1 and 2 star reviews here on Amazon that are almost comical in their dismissal of this film. And that's OK. If you were brought up on Bourne Identity or Batman, I can understand how Bullitt could seem  dated, anachronistic. But then, you'd have to say that the Maltese Falcon, from 28 years earlier still (1940), is also \\"outdated\\". Fine.<br /><br />Bullitt came out of a long tradition of Warner Brothers crime films, going way back to the 1930's. Warner's traditional theatrical distribution territory (before the breakup of the major studios) was the Midwest, and their story department was influenced (if influenced is even the word) by the organized crime culture that grew out of Prohibition. (An unusual period in American history from 1920-1932). Think Al Capone. Chicago.<br /><br />But no one had more empathy for the common man, or got down and dirty with crime and law enforcement, or understood life on the street, better than Warner Bros. They owned the tough-guy genre. Bogart and Cagney came out of that whole scene -- the tough loners. Car chases and gun shots were Warner Bros' stock in trade for many years. Anybody else that tried to muscle in on WB's tough-guy territory came across like wimps.<br /><br />But Bullitt elevated the detective genre to a new, hyper-realistic level. It was real this time, because it was all shot on location in San Francisco, which was a new thing in the 1960's. Developments in film, cameras, and changes in the studio system itself, made it more feasible to shoot on location than ever before. Steve McQueen insisted on it. Independent, auteur filmaking made this possible. He and director Peter Yates were the team that brought Bullitt to the peak of realism. (See special features on the disk for much more about this.)<br /><br />So as a result, Bullitt gets really, really immersed in San Francisco. Not just in the places, but the use of  non-actors after the shooting, in the hospital, and especially whenever police are on the scene, make this film maximally immersive in time and place. No acting in any film was ever more transparent, more real. I don't even know how you can call it acting, you need another verb to describe it. The background layer of the city itself really holds just as strong a pride of place as the human characters. The two fit each other like hand in glove.<br /><br />The visual style that Peter Yates and Director of Photography William Fraker have put together here is extremely important, and it's consistent with the way Steve McQueen looks around with his big pearly blues. From the beginning open title sequence, a \\"wandering follow focus\\" style of shooting puts a marvelous, exploratory depth into the layers of action, searching, scanning constantly for his suspect. The follow focus guy in Bullitt is an essential part of the cutting edge 1968 style, lending a freshness and spontaneity to all the locations.<br /><br />It all just seems to \\"happen\\". Because Bullitt is much more than just characters, events, or dialogue. Thanks to Peter Yates, Bullitt is a texture you can feel, it's a way of life in San Francisco in 1968 you can absorb in your bones. There's half-submerged dialogue that is thrown away, beautifully mumbled, improvised, or muffled in layers of caked blood. Bullitt seems to balance drama and documentary on a knife-edge. This, too, was new.<br /><br />You can feel the happening in the suburban ladies talking about pruning their rose gardens in Walter Chalmer's \\"home\\" -- which is so huge and extruded, it feels more like a public structure. You can sense it in the subtle hints of the emerging counter-culture, the improvised details of the city and the street. You can feel it in the friction between Bullitt's blue-collar world and Chalmers' privileged and patrician environment.<br /><br />So there it is: At the core of the story is class warfare, the nose-to-nose conflict between Chalmers and Bullitt. The search for the real Johnny Ross (the Mafia witness Chalmers wants to see in court on Monday morning) is the macguffin that facilitates that conflict. The hypocrisy of Chalmers is the role that immortalizes Robert Vaughn, not Napoleon Solo. Chalmers' fantasy of power is that he's going to \\"expose the organization\\"...but he ends up exposing himself. He IS the organization.<br /><br />And it is all so relevant today...the \\"juice\\" is there now more than ever, the smell of money, of influence in politics, and it permeates the whole system. Chalmers has his own people -- bright young law grads, police captains and the like, who get things done for him. And soon, Bullitt's job is on the line, he's under political pressure.<br /><br />Another theme, of course, was how far down into the \\"gutter\\" does a cop have to be to do his job? The conflict between Lieutenant Bullitt and girlfriend Cathy hinted at this issue, a theme that, like guns at the airport, now seems so naive. A cop is troubled by the moral boundaries of his work? Well, Popeye Doyle (The French Connection -- 1970) took care of that. Dirty Harry (1971) nailed that one, too. Dirty Harry was Clint Eastwood's direct answer to Frank Bullitt's moral dilemma. Harry Callahan was going to straighten out subversive San Francisco once and for all. (The subtext: Bullitt of course, wasn't going after \\"subversive\\", leftist San Francisco at all, but the nation's organized crime problem that FBI head J. Edgar Hoover had consistently failed to acknowledge for years.)<br /><br />But Cathy, Bullitt's woman, is sensitive enough to be troubled by violence. Actress Jacqueline Bissett is too pretty to belong in this picture, but she contrasts so nicely with Frank Bullitt's hard-boiled world, and brings a touch of culture that, along with everything else, turns Frank's life into a high-wire act. And makes it a life worth living.<br /><br />The bone-crunching violence was real, too, as blood started to flow on the screen as it never had before. Bullitt was never for the faint of heart. The moment that discreet, professional hit-man (John Aprea) slammed Albert Renick with the Winchester Pump in the dingy room at the Hotel Daniels, crime films would never be the same. The low source music on the radio was SO MUCH a part of that terrifying experience, like a narcotic dose of a pain-killer, soothing yet eerily indifferent as life drained out of the victim.<br /><br />But if Schifrin's source music worked in favor of the realism, the score worked against it. The big-band elements of Lalo Schifrin's score carbon-date Bullitt, and at times the bluesy big band is overbearing. The preamble to the car chase was overdone, announcing too brashly, \\"get ready for something big.\\"<br /><br />But I crave the cold, edgy string clusters (such as those that open the film), the percussive, tension-inducing claves and shakers. Rock N Roll wasn't ready for this scoring assignment. Rock could only subvert itself in a crime film, become part of the crime itself. And Bernard Herrmann or Jerry Goldsmith wouldn't have been right, either. No way. Somehow, Schifrin bridged the musical gap between cops and culture in a new and very personal way.<br /><br />So much has been said about the car chase, which has been imitated and expanded upon, from movies like Vanishing Point, to the Seven Ups, Ronin, Live and Die in LA, and on and on. Of course the classic Shelby Mustang vs Dodge Charger confrontation has since been \\"eclipsed\\". The Bullitt car chase is the sequence that other action films and teams of stuntmen will measure themselves against, always.<br /><br />But what blows minds too, is the gun battle and the final chase inside the airline terminal, from the days before metal detectors and x-rays. Can anyone born after 1970 even imagine bringing a gun on an aircraft? That is totally insane. But that is the way it was. Security for the time was extremely lax, but those circumstances wouldn't last much longer.<br /><br />The money really shows on the screen when you've got men running amongst jet airplanes, shooting at each other on a real airport tarmac, weaving in and out of luggage carts. Wow, how did they do that?? Men actually DO sweat, my friends. The trembling shriek of the offscreen woman just after Ross has been blown away by Bullitt through the pneumatic doors -- he's lying there in a pool of his own blood -- is among the most startlingly real sounds in any film, ever. How did they DO that?? Who IS that troubled woman? She's not an actress.<br /><br />Bullitt has not only gritty realism, but stark, cutting-edge design which starts with those simply incredible opening titles. Amidst an abstract tableau of frozen extras pretending to be mannequins, tear gas, screeching tires, and broken glass, Pablo Ferro's beautiful, flowing \\"negative space\\" title wipes and Lalo Schifrin's latin-jazz main title merge to create the most graceful, utterly cool open title sequence in all of cinema.<br /><br />You can't deny those open titles are Bond-influenced. The up and down minor guitar notes of the Bullitt theme, and the exquisite opticals owe some debt, I think, to 007.<br /><br />McQueen as Bullitt is soulful and woeful, subdued and pensive. It's a counter-intuitive way to play against what movie detectives had traditionally done: talk, ask questions, and talk some more. Frank Bullitt doesn't make any speeches -- you can leave that to Chalmers and the rest of the 1 percent. Bullitt's strategy to solve this crime is to shut up, hide the dead body and let other people ask the questions.<br /><br />Supporting members of the cast are unforgettable, including Don Gordon as Delgetti, Simon Oakland is pitch perfect as Captain Bennet, Pat Renella and Felice Orlandi (the confusing \\"Ross\\" twins), Norman Fell, Georg Stanford Brown, Carl Reindel. Real, real, real. How did they make it so REAL???<br /><br />In short, every crime film ever made since Bullitt has become about male goaling -- the burning desire to achieve a goal, no matter what the cost. Sex and violence are the very shaping forces of every species on earth. They aren't just tools of dramatic artifice. To survive, you always have to push the envelope to a new level. That's what Bullitt does best.anized crime problem that FBI head J. Edgar Hoover had consistently failed to acknowledge for years.) 0316017930|4.0|A recent good read, which changed my perspective on what it takes to become a wild success in life. The Outliers is about how success is affected by external factors beyond individual characteristics. These factors include culture, social systems, even friends and families. Here are some highlights:  1. The Matthew Effect - Canadian professional hockey players are mostly born in the early months of a year because the age cutoff for tryouts is January 1st. These players when trying out during childhood, have months of physical development over their later-born counterparts. This is a huge advantage which gives successful candidates access to premium coaches and programmes for years, hence an even greater perceived gap compared with others. Hence this anomaly when the survey was performed.  Similar patterns are found in Major League Baseball, English Premier League, among other sports leagues.  Even when it comes to school, kids born later in the year are at a slight disadvantage.  Hard to believe, but this arbitrary external factor (cutoff dates) affects people in a very deep way.  2. 10 000-Hour Rule - it generally takes someone 10 000 hours of practice to be an expert at something. Mozart did not produce his first masterpiece until 10 years into his career. The Beatles had years of practice doing small performances in Germany before reaching super stardom. Bill Joy, Bill Gates and Steve Jobs had all that early-year access to computers before they became visionaries in technology.  3. Being Sufficiently Smart - you do not need to be a genius to be successful. In fact, many geniuses have failed to succeed as they did not receive the necessary \\"cultivation\\" during their minor years. As long as you are smart enough (IQ of around 120), you can be successful if your are raised properly. Good to know that an IQ higher than 120 does not buy you any more success.   4.Cultural Influence - your roots have much more to do with certain kinds of success or failure. Korean Air had a bad rap on high accident rates years ago. It turned out Koreans' indirect way of interacting with figures of authority, i.e., pilots, control towers, have everything to do with this. Asians tend to excel at math because our language makes it easier to cognitively manipulate numbers. Asian cultures usually treasure the value of working hard in life, which knowingly or not, helps them succeed in a subject that requires ample practice!  This book provides some encouraging takeaways for the not-so-genius people like me.  For one, given the right cultivation, we have an equally good chance to succeed as the Einsteins of the world. Take a relatively intelligent person, give him the right exposure and fostering during youth and adolescence coupled with a belief in hard work and persistence, remove the negative external factors that are rooted in your culture or circumstances, and voila, you have a formula to manufacture success!  -PTS

B003S897U0|5.0|Ah the Crowes. Such throwbacks -- 20 years ago (my god, has it really been that long?) they were reveling in raucous retro-roots-rock, when everyone else was recording ballads on all-acoustic albums. Times change, and the Crowes have too, but they are still about 20 years behind with Croweology, the obligatory \\"classics live mostly-acoustic\\" album.   Funny thing is, the idea actually works. This is one epic, meandering joy ride of an album with something for everyone, even only casual fans. The professional reviewers want to talk about a \\"ragged\\" sound, which I suppose is accurate, if a little misleading. What Croweology really feels like is a very well produced live album -- recorded by a band that certainly still knows how to ride a groove when they find one, and they riff over these classics with gusto. Some are hits, some are favorites, and some are also-rans that here have new coats of paint.  The versions of hits like Jealous Again, Remedy, She Talks To Angels, and Soul Singing that appear here are really well done alternate takes, and I actually rather prefer this album's version of a couple of them to the originals. The renditions of Share the Ride, Non-Fiction, Hotel Illness, Morning Song, and Sister Luck are fantastic, tight grooves. The band also stretches out into indulgent extended jams on Ballad of Urgency, Wiser Time and Thorn in My Pride -- all three come in at over 9 minutes, which seems horribly long on paper, but somehow doesn't get old when listening.   If there's a weak spot, it's at the very end... the version of Bad Luck Blue Eyes included here is kind of sub-standard -- which is a shame because Bad Luck has long been one of my favorites. I'm not really surprised though -- I've heard several alternate recordings of this one, and none are as good as the original. The problem seems to be, here as always, that Chris Robinson just shreds his voice early on, and doesn't have anything left by the conclusion. I give them a miss on this one, but an album that should go out with a bang, sort of goes out with a whimper.  In short, Croweology is a wonderful album, and has the feel of a \\"live\\" performance in more ways than one... in this era of albums produced as a loosely-bound collection of downloadable singles, Croweology really works as an album in the traditional sense. The tracks flow together well, particularly the break between Ballad of Urgency and Wiser Time -- these run together almost like one long track. There's no need for shuffle-mode here. Overall, an excellent album, a treat for big fans like me, enjoyable for casual fans like my wife, and a decent hook for those who are unfamiliar with the band

1586487981|5.0|I enjoyed this one a lot. Felt very informative and had the right pace to introduce me to something I knew very little about.  I had naively assumed that the poor of the world just lack stuff (money, clothing, medicine, food), but that's not the case.  They do lack some of those things, but there are interesting political/human reasons that simply supplying the stuff doesn't always work.  In poor countries there's: often entrenched political corruption that keeps aid from arriving to those who need it; there are interesting and counterintuitive reasons related to innate human irrationality that certain approaches don't work (for instance, supplying free mosquito nets in Africa causes people to value them less); the prospect of getting money (loans) to the poor is difficult due to the cost of managing the small amounts of money involved and thus has created a middle market of moneylenders at exorbitant rates; micro-loans like Kiva (Kiva is not mentioned specifically in the book, but other micro-loan orgs are) do help but are not a cure-all; the One Laptop per Child approach (also not mentioned directly in the book) used to seem like a panacea to me, but after reading this I think it shoots far above the head.  Education is a big topic in the book, at all levels: both primary (through schooling), but there are also analyses of how the lack of education of the populace on the benefits of, say, medicine, clean water, and micronutrients all contribute to difficulty solving those problems.  I like the approach of this book a lot:  1) Describe a problem (e.g., lack of food), 2) use established research to evaluate the effectiveness of different approaches, 3) hypothesize the underlying human motivations that influence the effectiveness of those approaches.  Steps 2 or 3 usually have some fascinating insights into human behavior that are obvious but unexpected.  After reading this book, I'm also much more interested in how I personally can help reduce worldwide poverty.

B001EOOQEM|5.0|The verdict is in...and the 1996 UK Castle remaster wins!!!<br />  I have done a Hi-Fi(and Lo-Fi MP3 player) comparison between the 2008 Rhino and the 1996 UK castle remaster....the Castle remaster wins!<br /><br /> Well, on the Rhino label release it sounds like engineers attempted to add punch and volume to the mid-bass 1.0 KHZ range and they succeeded...BUT at the same time muddied the treble clarity at around the 6.0 KHZ-10 KHZ range. Normally I like juiced-up remasters, but this one sounds forced and strained in the Mid-Bass. Imagine your speakers cranked at high volume with bass all the way up..now throw a pillow over the speaker...  It's so bad that you'll have listener fatigue and become annoyed by the second song.<br />  That's how the Rhino sounds to me.<br /><br />  The Castle remaster as compared to the original 1980 recording has been remastered with great balance and clarity. The bass drum and bass guitar have added \\"whump\\" without the muddiness of the Rhino version. Now keep in mind, the bass drum/tom recording style of the time (1980)was with alot of dampening, so don't expect it to sound too \\"Fat\\". It was intended to sound hard and tight.<br />The higher frequencies have been opened up with a wider soundstage. Dio's lyrics are much easier to understand and Tony's guitar blazes just right (especially on the guitar solos of the title track #4).<br /><br />  ...and if that wasn't enough!...the liner notes have been expanded with LYRICS!! and a short history surrounding the recording of this groundbreaking Heavy Metal recording.<br /><br />  It took me two tries to get the 1996 Import I was looking for, so do yourself a favor and e-mail the seller beforehand to verify just what version they have. The seller I found mine at was nice enough to send photos of the CD case so there was no doubt which one he was sending. **Thanks Trader Jack's!**<br /><br />  To know what the CD case looks like, look up the Heaven and Hell title and open up the long list of \\"Formats\\". Select the 1996 import version and when the user photos come up under the main photo, you'll see a photo of what the back of the Castle remaster looks like (the front is the same). It is different than the 1980 version and instead has a live on-stage photo of the band and make sure at the bottom of the back of the case it says 1996 Gimcastle Ltd/Castle Communications.<br /><br />  Simply put,the Castle remaster of Mob Rules sounds just right and I give it my highest recommendation.<br /><br /> For those of you who are just discovering \\"Dio\\" style Black Sabbath...it's a MUST BUY TITLE!!!<br /><br />****\\"I'll give you a star so you'll know just where you are.\\"****Formats\\". 0670022950|5.0|I found this book absorbing and fascinating.  It brings together insights from world history, neuroscience, social psychology, and sociology to hammer home a provocative and optimistic thesis:  the world has moved away from violence, for reasons that are comprehensible and may, with luck, continue to impact the world.  The arguments are based on data and are convincing.  I'll admit I particularly relish evidence indicating a world where women have more power will be more peaceful.  The writing is leavened with both wit and humanity. It crackles with intelligence and is a delight to read--so much so that this long, intellectually challenging book was a true page-turner for me.<br /><br />What I could have done without...well, I am not an atheist though I'm not a religious dogmatist either.  When the author writes proudly about not being tempted to ascribe positive changes to any divine guiding hand, I'm not impressed.  Instead, I'm disconcerted to find him suddenly leaping from views solidly based on empirical evidence, to an opinion resting on no possible factual knowledge--his starry-eyed faith that there is no divine order.  His take on the Bible is, to put it mildly, one-sided (I admit it's also hilarious and he doesn't make anything up).  Anyone reading it who had never read the text in question would wonder why any sane person would take an oath with his hand on what is described as an account of murder and savagery.  The idea that the Bible contains notions that were ethically advanced for their time is nowhere acknowledged.<br /><br />Why the five stars if the atheistic stuff left me underwhelmed?  Because this is such a good book overall--the vivid and brilliant treatment of history alone is worth five stars.  I would look to another book to dispassionately examine the role of different kinds of religious faith in instigating or preventing violence.  I would certainly look elsewhere to learn about how committed Christians struggled to abolish slavery.  But I believe Pinker is right in his evaluation of the humane impact of the ideas of the Enlightenment. (Parenthetically, I have to say I found the author a little harsh in his treatment of Elizabeth I.  She was a lot more tolerant and less murderous than her dad or her sister--whom he lumps her with--and after all Mary Queen of Scots, the relative she executed, tried to assassinate her.  Elizabeth bravely faught a defensive war against Spain--the Spain of the Inquisition.  But overall Pinker's reading of history seems judicious.)<br /><br />I got this book on my Kindle.  There are negatives about that.  It's hard to make out some of the charts and a couple of places a bit of a sentence is repeated.  The index is useless.  If I were a scholar who was using this book as a source I would get it in print.  For a general reader the pros outweigh the cons, at least for me--it's cheaper, you can get it instantaneously without schlepping it home from a bookstore, and a big positive--this is TEXT TO SPEECH ENABLED.  Meaning if your eyes get tired or if you need to be physically busy, you can listen to it.  For a long book like this which is so interesting you won't want to be pried away from it, that is worth a whole lot.right in his evaluation of the humane impact of the ideas of the Enlightenment. B004RFYEEC|5.0|It is difficult to accurately judge this set, simply because I am so shocked it actually has been released.  While much of this material was leaked out on bootlegs over the past few decades (as well as the first official release of material on the band's box set in the early 90's), we finally have a legitimate release of a deluge of music from the aborted album's sessions.<br /><br />First, the album itself.  Using Wilson's 2004 solo release as its template, this does not entirely work as a stand-alone piece as many of the sections used here were not completed at the time of the original album sessions.  That said, it is still astonishing to hear a straight runthrough of the closest \\"SMiLE\\" came to completion.  And the vast majority of what is finished, and even most of what was not finished, is deeply awe-inspiring.  That it was so close to potentially being finalized it its own time just makes the album's history that much more bittersweet.<br /><br />Now let's get to the four additional CDs of sessions.  Simply put, these are a godsend.  It is fascinating to be a fly on the wall of these sessions, and the roughly 5 hours of material here was well chosen and carefully culled from the overwhelming amount of material on the master tapes.  There is enough material on this set to intimidate even the most ardent Beach Boys fans, and yet it is incredibly well-organized, with multiple takes and disparate elements grouped by song (in album order) and then in roughly chronological order.<br /><br />It is fascinating to hear tracks mutate over the course of multiple takes--sometimes into different songs.  \\"Wonderful\\", for example, originally started out similar to the finished track, before taking a truly bizarre detour into an entirely different song (something akin to doo wop with lyrics saying \\"Come on pretty baby and rock with me Henry), only to eventually revert to how it was initially.  You'll also hear discussions and a great many experiments, all of which are fascinating snapshots of the spirit of the times and these sessions.<br /><br />I don't want to give away too many of the surprises to be found here, but these were some of my favorite discoveries from the sessions tracks:<br /><br />- Creepy vocals done for the barnyard/animals section of \\"Heroes & Villains\\"<br />- The track which tends to show up alternately as \\"Tones\\" and \\"Tune X\\" on bootlegs really is from the \\"SMiLE\\" sessions, was written by Carl, and sounds pretty great when sourced from the master tapes<br />- Brian using the final 15 minutes of session time available with an orchestra to improvise a number inexplicably titled \\"Three - Blind Mice\\" (no relation to the children's song)<br />- Bizarre skits where Brian acts out being trapped in a piano, and then in a microphone<br />- Brian talking the horn players through getting the \\"talking horns\\" sound he wants for \\"Surf's Up\\"<br />- A chorus of Brians doing a multi-part harmony for Vega-tables<br />- The jazz improvisation that leads into a take of \\"I Want to Be Around\\"<br />- In between takes of \\"Our Prayer\\", Brian asking if the others \\"feel the acid kicking in\\"<br />- Eight-and-a-half minutes of the infamous \\"Fire\\" recordings<br />- A slightly jazzy instrumental take of \\"Da Da\\" on electric and regular pianos, that would have worked well in an early Peanuts cartoons<br />- and then the hilariously sarcastic intros to the next two takes of the same<br />- A great many variations of the different parts of Good Vibrations, many of which are quite significantly different from the passages used in the released version<br />- A Capitol promo ad telling retailers \\"SMiLE\\" will be released in January of '67, and how it is going to sell a million copies.  This is like a communication from some strange parallel universe where it really was released at that time<br /><br />Ridiculously extensive session notes often include session personnel and instruments played, the date, and usually the time, of each recording, master numbers (where available) as well as information regarding takes that were likely taped over or eventually stolen.  These are found in the 60-page hardcover book that accompanies the set.  My thanks to the designers for making a book of these dimensions a hardcover as it would otherwise be unwieldly.<br /><br />This book is half of the total experience.  It is a treasure trove of photos, quotes, reminiscences, lyrics, artwork and liner notes on a wide range of topics, and from a variety of viewpoints, as Wilson and Van Dyke Parks provide introductions, each of the Beach Boys give their own insights, Peter Reum relates the signifigance of this work, Domenic Priore provides a timeline of events and an essay on the relation of band to their peers at the time, Mark Linett gives an overview of the production of this set, and Frank Holmes describes the process of producing his imagery for the original booklet (which is also included in the 2 LP vinyl set contained here).<br /><br />And all of this comes in a box that enhances the cover art by setting the various elements at different depths, turning the originally 2D SMiLE shop into a 3D diorama.  While this component of the packaging surely added to the total cost, I think it is a nice touch and it give an unconcious indicator of what you are about to experience when opening the lid:  \\"SMiLE\\" is about to become a reality, with deeper dimensions than it ever had in previous incarnations.<br /><br />I find myself dismayed by the trend towards increasingly over-indulgent (which translates to overly-expensive) boxed sets, but here is one that truly warrants the cost.  Unlike some other recent deluxe sets, this set is truly an immersive experience--a combination of music, art and text that will be keeping me fully engaged for weeks on end, and without any unnecessary trinkets that cheapen the legacy of that music.<br /><br />A complete win.stolen. Johnny K's interpretation of MEGADETH's brand of chaos is especially note worthy here.  6.  NEVER DEAD:  \\"That's a video game track, they did that already with GEARS OF WAR,\\" you critics whine.  Suck it up and up the volume.  The technichality present on this track shows a band not afraid to put itself out there.  This song is seamless and aggressive with a rhythm that draws you in and keeps your headbanging attention.   7.  NEW WORLD ORDER:  \\"Another one we heard already,\\" I hear you say, and again your voice does not carry.  I loved this tune from the start, it only got better here and the studio treatment it deserved.  Johnny K, whatever you did to the mix here, keep up the great work.  This song finds its rightful place here on TH1RT3EN.  8.  FAST LANE:  Wow, this song jumps out at you.  The build up to the chorus and the leads and fills are all over the place, a beautiful chaotic, melodic masterpiece.  Great snarling Dave lyrics going on here.  9.  BLACK SWAN:  My third favorite track.  Dave's lyrics mean much to me and will claw your soul open if you have one.  Awesome enhancement of the intro and mix as compared to the much beloved demo version.  10.  WRECKER:  My second WOW of the album, not a woe like some you critics I read so far.  I love the lyrics.  This song made me smile because it quite simply rocked.  Thats all I need to say here.  11.  MILLENNIUM OF THE BIND:  My Second favorite track; everything I said about NEW WORLD ORDER times 13!  The mix, the additions, the sheer glory of it.  Thanks Dave for getting this one out of mothballs.  12.  DEADLY NIGHTSHADE:  A stand alone mosher, a super nova track on an album of glorious morning stars.  This track will go underrated by many critics, but do yourself a favor and do not listen to the critics.  Turn this one up and rattle your head!  13.  13:  My favorite...Tear my heart out rebuild it with metal shards and add lyrics sharper than a two-edged sword and extremely personal.  DAVE, DAVID,CHRIS, SHAWN, VIC, MEGADETH, we love you and this song bleeds your love for your work and for your fans.  As for my criticism of critics, no hard feelings, thanks for reading this review and give yourself over to an extreme highlight of the journey that is MEGADETH!  METAL BLESSINGS TO ALL!!!!

B005LRWRZ8|3.0|I've been a hardcore Megadeth fan for two decades now and being a singer/lyricist/guitarist have been greatly influenced by them and tend to dissect each album differently than everyone else I know. That being said, I knew not to expect \\"Endgame 2\\", but this truly surprised me. Dave scaled the leads on this album WAAAAY back, choosing instead to focus on the rhythm primarily, then riffs, which in and of itself wouldn't be so bad if most of it didn't sound like he was blatantly ripping himself off. Doesn't help that Shawn & David (welcome back) only give slightly better than mediocre performances. Don't get me wrong, the axework is superb, but great playing doesn't necessarily make a great song.     The lyrics are exactly what you've come to expect, but decidedly more \\"World Needs A Hero\\" - \\"United Abominations\\" than \\"Endgame\\" quality. Some are well crafted gold, some are unapologetic expressions from the heart and some just seem settled upon as, \\"good enough, let's move on\\". A little disappointing, at least from a lyricist's standpoint. I do like the way the basic structure of each track varies, something that I try to do with my own stuff and showing that the musician wants that song to be unique unto itself, if not the best that it can be. Verse, chorus, repeat, solo, etc. get's stale and more artists should care about changing it up. The fact that it's done so well here is an example of the caliber of musicianship that has come to define Mustaine. Respect.  As other reviewers have said, some of the tracks are re-hashes of '90's era ideas & demos, some of which I think were added just to get the song count up to 13. \\"New World Order\\" & \\"Millenium Of The Blind\\" were both on the 2004 re-release of \\"Youthanasia\\", the former of which I've been listening to for over a decade. When I first saw the track listing months ago I thought, \\"No. Did he? Why would he?\\". But he did. Aside from changing maybe 3 notes it's just the demo re-recorded. Same with \\"Millenium\\". Filler tracks? For shame, Dave. \\"Sudden Death\\" was in a Guitar Hero game (or Rock Band, who cares?) a while back and \\"Never Dead\\" was recorded as the theme to an upcoming game of the same name, but those are much more recent and forgivable. Though I must say, we never saw any tracks from \\"Hidden Treasures\\" or any of the other misc. songs make their way to any other studio album before now except for \\"Dread & The Fugitive Mind\\" (\\"Capitol Punishment\\"/\\"World Needs A Hero\\"). Why not another \\"Silent Scorn\\" or some short interlude tracks? A least they'd be NEW SONGS.  This is a good album, but certainly not great. I'm sure in time I'll love it to death, but it'll take a lot of getting used to. For new fans: This should be no one's introduction to Megadeth. For vets: You'll dig it. Maybe more, maybe less, because I know only I can hear through my ears, and you might hear it differently. Despite my focusing on the negative aspects, it's well worth the $12 amazon wants. I've done my part, now the decision is yours.

0525423656|4.0|Crossed has a different feel when compared to Matched. The story takes place largely outside of The Society on the edges and there is the constant threat of danger and a struggle to survive. Another change is that Crossed is written from both Cassia and Ky's perspectives in alternating chapters. What I liked about this change is that we could learn more about Ky and gain a better understanding of his personality. Some of his past is revealed and it is very sad. I sometimes preferred his chapters to Cassia's. New characters were also introduced and one in particular appears to have secrets. Indie, one of these new characters, is a nice foil for Cassia as she is an assertive and independent girl with an \\"act now, think later\\" attitude.   Something else I liked about Crossed was that we finally get to see that there are people who did not blindly follow The Society like little sheep. In Matched it seemed like everyone went along with the rules but now we see the history and that there was a group protesting the changes. When I read the first book, I couldn't help but wonder why no one protested the ridiculous rules and way of life when it was first implemented (by now the younger generations are brainwashed into accepting it) but now I see that people did protest. The majority of those who grew up in The Society like Cassia, never knew about the dissenters because it was hushed up and the people involved were either killed or they escaped. I am eager to find out more about the uprising and who the leader is.   Just like with Matched, there are literary references throughout Crossed. The book takes its name from the poem \\"Crossing the Bar\\" by Alfred Lord Tennyson. \\"Crossing The Bar\\" is used as a code for those seeking to join the uprising as the Pilot is believed to refer to the leader. It also refers to the characters crossing to safety. The importance of poetry, art, and music is woven throughout the novel. The Society places more value on technology and productivityrather than creativity but people still long for that. There is even a black market where you can trade poems to get what you want. This theme seems to be a strong pro-arts message that resonates today with budget cuts leading to schools eliminating or reducing art and music programs.   Overall I think Crossed is a good sequel to Matched though I had some issues with it. There are parts of the story that seemed too slow and at other times I wanted more explanation, particularly about the rebellion, but it wasn't provided. I suppose that I will have to wait for the answers in book three. I was also annoyed by the love triangle though Xander is not present for most of the novel. I know that this will be resolved in the final book so it is just a slight problem. I liked that Cassia is becoming more determined and starting to think for herself though she does still have her moments. I also liked the increased action and suspense in the plot. Crossed may not have the excellent world building of Matched but it does a nice job of developing the characters and setting up the story for its conclusion which I am sure will be thrilling.

B005LRWRZ8|4.0|Megadeth's Thirteen is like a Countdown to Extinction/Youthanasia hybrid with bits of Endgame thrown in.  It's not exactly what I was hoping for even though it still boasts high quality musicianship. I think I would give this album a three if it was a lesser band than Megadeth, and almost did anyway.  While it does offer many good moments, it grows progressively weaker, with all the highlights coming in the first half and too many slow songs throughout the album.  I've been listening to Megadeth for many, many years, and have always thought their musical ideas sounded far more interesting when they've been at least somewhat technical and complex.  When they simplify the formula and shoot for mid-paced or slower hard rock, many of the songs get boring to me after just a few listens.  Unfortunately, over half of Thirteen falls into this category, as Dave Mustaine seems to be trying to recreate his commercial glory days of the 90's.  Personally, I think it's a bad move considering the fact that their last album, Endgame, and their participation in the Big 4 shows had seemed to reenergize a shrinking fan base.  Let's face it, the 90's are over; metal fans today don't want simplistic, unchallenging music, and mainstream hard rock fans don't want anything close to real metal, so this leaves Megadeth without strong support from either side for an album like Thirteen.  Even though their greatness shines through on several tracks, the average outweighs the strong here, making for an unsatisfying release from a band that was on the right track with their last album, but now takes a small step backward.  I wish Amazon had a half-star option because it's not quite as bad as a 3, but not quite as strong as a 4, at least by Megadeth standards.<br /><br />Thirteen does have many good moments.  Opener Sudden Death sounds like a mix of Peace Sells/Rust in Peace style thrash, except for the Cryptic Writings-sounding chorus.  The song features great soloing, above-average vocals from Mustaine, and great riffing.  It's a good song, but the chorus doesn't completely gel with the verses.  Whose Life Is It (Anyways) is an alright track, though the guitar tone doesn't sound much like Megadeth and the main riff is very simple.  The bridge sounds a lot like Youthanasia, but the vocals and beats are reminiscent of So Far, So Good, So What, especially the chorus.  It's a fast song, but not at all technical or thrashy.  We the People has a Youthanasia vibe similar to Black Curtains.  Hard rock with a metal groove.  It has a catchy riff and vocal melodies that will probably stick and strong songwriting that is a bit more technical than anything from Youthanasia.  Never Dead might be my favorite track on the CD.  It's pure Peace Sells-era thrash, with a great verse riff and vintage vocals.  The chorus sounds like one of the mid-paced ones from Rust in Peace, which works with the Peace Sells riffing in the verses.  It's too bad more of the album wasn't like this track.  The last highlight for me is the song Fast Lane, which has a Countdown to Extinction vibe; post-thrash heavy metal.  It's a good song, but not quite a great one.  It will probably end up being one of the better tracks on the album, which speaks to the fact that Thirteen is much more lightweight than Endgame.<br /><br />Now for the complaints.  Public Enemy #1, the second track, has one of the album's many less-than-incredible choruses.  It's a subdued, mid-paced metal/rock song coming right on the heels of a faster, heavy opener.  A few tracks later, you have Guns, Drugs, and Money, which sounds like a throwaway filler song.  It attempts to replicate the simplistic approach of Youthanasia with far less effectiveness than We the People.  The verse riff is simple and boring, and the vocals are not good.  Skip ahead a couple more tracks, and you get several redone, re-worked, re-recorded, whatever you want to call it, songs from Megadeth's past.  New World Order sounds slightly more polished than the version on the Youthanasia re-master, but the vocals are erratic at best.  I'd prefer the new music with the old vocals.  On Youthanasia, the song was an o.k. bonus track, and here, it's an o.k. album track.  Not great or even really good, just o.k.  The thrash section that finishes it off isn't bad, but this isn't anything you can really call a highlight.  Black Swan is even less impressive.  I haven't heard the original version, but this one, despite a catchy chorus and decent soloing, has boring riffs and forgettable verses.  A third older song, Millennium of the Blind, adds several parts to the original version and has more of a metal vibe than the songs around it, but just isn't strong enough to qualify as a \\"heavy moment,\\" for which it seems to be intended.  Moving on, Wrecker is barely heavier than Youthanasia, with an AC/DC-sounding riff in parts and similar subject matter (about a home-wrecking woman).  No thrash whatsoever, and not very memorable.  Deadly Nightshade opens with a riff that sound much closer to Metallica's Death Magnetic than Dave would probably like.  The song is mid-paced groove, a mix of Countdown/Youthanasia with riffs that sound rehashed from several past Megadeth albums.  It has some decent guitar work near the end, but it's nowhere near amazing, adding to the mediocrity of the album's second half.  Just too many songs that are only o.k.  The title track finishes things off in that vein, with yet another autobiographical summary of Dave's career set to semi-heavy, slow-to-mid-paced hard rock.  It's not bad, but it seems like Megadeth is capable of much more.<br /><br />I wish I had better things to say about Thirteen, which I had high hopes for after Endgame.  I know some fans felt this release was being rushed when it was first announced, and I'm sure its hit-and-miss nature won't do anything to answer those claims.  Megadeth is a great band, and their music is better than much of today's more popular \\"metal,\\" but I'm disappointed with this effort because so many of the songs are just good, not great.  Hopefully they'll let tracks like Sudden Death and Never Dead guide them in coming up with material for the next one.Youthanasia re-master, but the vocals are erratic at best.  I'd prefer the new music with the old vocals.  On Youthanasia, the song was an o.k. bonus track, and here, it's an o.k. album track.  Not great or even really good, just o.k. Hopefully they'll let tracks like Sudden Death and Never Dead guide them in coming up with material for the next one.

B005NPLXJ4|2.0|So Lou Reed has this idea kicking around for a few years that he wants to write music for a German theatre group based on a series of old plays about an abused dancer who crosses paths with Jack the Ripper.  He can't get it to sound how he wants, but then he jams with Metallica at a Rock and Roll Hall of Fame show.  Eureka!  Lou finds some young, hip rockers (compared to him, anyway) who can give him that dark, dangerous atmosphere he's been looking for.  According to both sides, he casually says, \\"Hey, let's do an album together,\\" after the jam and goes on his way.  And now, two years later, we have Lulu.  After listening to the album in its entirety, I have to wonder if Lou proposed this mess honestly when he brought it back up.  I wonder if he said, \\"Hey, guys, are you willing to jump the shark and alienate the majority of your fan base (again) to be on an album with me?\\"  And `Tallica, ever the experimenters, say, \\"Sure, Lou.  We cranked out a decent metal album a couple years ago that went #1 around the world and energized millions of our fans, but hey, you're Lou Reed!  Count us in!\\"  I'm not complaining because I'm narrow-minded or don't like variety or risk-taking.  The problem here is that, musically, vocally, and lyrically, repetition is the general idea behind these songs.  Find a basic riff and just repeat it for anywhere from 4 to 20 minutes.  Add in some noise and feedback here and there for at least some dynamics, and let Lou talk/\\"sing\\"/ramble over it until he's done.  I know this is more of a Lou Reed album than a Metallica one, but whoever it belongs to, it's not very interesting or listenable.  Considering the negative attention it's drawn, I'm not sure if many people will spring for this, but then again, since it is Metallica, it will probably sell millions of copies anyway.  Oh well.  This album will almost surely end up being just as much an anomaly as other odd forays into metal by the likes of Pat Boone and William Shatner, remembered more for its novelty than its quality.  Now, for anyone still interested, here's a track-by-track breakdown.<br /><br />Brandenburg  Gate--Very, very repetitive music and rambling from Lou; terrible vocals from both Reed and Hetfield, with Reed doing his talking/poetry reading/embarrassing random stabs at singing, and Het yodeling, \\"Small town guh-r-rrl!\\" throughout.<br /><br />The View--Simple, repetitive riff that goes nowhere; harsh, aggressive vocals from Het are somewhat surprising and hopefully hint at where he's going on the next Metallica album.  However, this song has the most ridiculed lyric so far; Het hilariously reminds us over and over that he is the table, making many listeners question his sanity (I know, the lyrics are from Lulu's POV, but it still sounds hilarious).  Lou's talking doesn't sound interesting, the boring riff doesn't sound interesting; what's to keep a person listening?<br /><br />Pumping Blood--Reed sounds ridiculous repeating the track's title over the opening riff.  The riff sounds like a prelude to some actual rocking...instead it just repeats again and again, then eventually goes into some ambient Metalli-noise over more Reed rambling.  Not something that will call out to be heard again.  The riff at the end of the song was the first time I found anything remotely interesting.  Even still, without Reed's talking, it would sound like Metallica just fooling around with their instruments.<br /><br />Mistress Dread--Starts with a riff that's faster and better than anything from Death Magnetic, but then it's ruined by Lou's attempts at singing, which are unlistenable, and constant repetition.  Seriously, the riff gets very boring after hearing it about two thousand times in a row.  No direction to this whatsoever.<br /><br />Iced Honey--Only track so far that starts out sounding like an actual song.  It's pretty bland, though.  Very simplistic hard rock track.  If they release a single, this will most likely be it; it's only about four-and-a-half minutes long, and has actual structure.  Unfortunately, it's forgettable.<br /><br />Cheat on Me--Ambient guitar sounds, in parts a bit Irish or Celtic-sounding...not bad to have in the background, until Reed comes in.  It would be different if he sounded good, but what he does vocally doesn't sound like it requires talent.  A track of mostly sounds, with a decent hard rock riff used here and there.  Some pretty bad vocals from Het.  This is the first track that I might actually like if it was an instrumental.  Musically, it builds up nicely to a fairly heavy ending.  Vocals from both singers ruin the song.<br /><br />Frustration--Reed's vocals border on ludicrous on this track; it feels more like a joke than music.  Anxiety-filled ambient noise goes into a Sabbath-inspired doomy riff that doesn't sound too bad, but Lou sure does.  Again, if this was an instrumental, it would be much more tolerable; not great, but tolerable.  It rocks out admirably near the end of the song, but there's almost a straight rip-off of the riff from C.O.C.'s Paranoid Opioid at 7:14 and 7:48.<br /><br />Little Dog--At this point, after seven songs and about 48 minutes, it's all starting to sound the same.  Some low-key acoustic playing, ambient background noise, Lou talking/rambling, sometimes trying to sing a few notes seemingly at random, a somewhat heavy hard rock riff.  It gets a bit redundant this far in, and there's still over half an hour left after this song.  I'm ready to do something else now, which is not a good sign when I'm listening to a new album.<br /><br />Dragon--Beginning doesn't sound any different from the last song.  Now it's getting extremely redundant...what seemed random during the first half now seems formulaic...some noise, throw in a riff, back to some noise.  Lou talks, tries to sing, goes back to talking.  Mighty boring.  Once the riff kicks in, it sounds like a Load throwaway.  There's a heavier riff in the second half of the song, but it sounds tired.<br /><br />Junior Dad--This one could sum up the whole album; 19 long minutes of the same formula; acoustic, noise, riff, repeat.  With almost twenty minutes to fill, though, Junior Dad goes long on the noise, with minutes-long sections of droning guitar sounds.  It's not that it sounds particularly bad, it just gets boring after so much repetition.  Maybe if an instrumental version comes out, I'll give it another chance, but for now I don't expect to be listening to Lulu again anytime soon.<br /><br />I know these guys are Rock n' Roll Hall of Famers and all, but both artists involved have done far better in their careers.  I have no problem with them doing whatever they want to do.  It's their right and they've earned it.  However, this is a review of the music on the album, not the artists' careers, and the music is not very interesting from a metal/ hard rock fan's perspective.  There are rare moments (and I do mean rare) when Lou's vocal style actually complements the music, and for a few seconds it works.  But those moments are fleeting, and the rest of the time it just doesn't even sound serious.  I don't blame Metallica; I'm sure if Mick Jagger asked them to play music for him on an album, they'd do it in a heartbeat, just to say they played all of the music on an album from a rock legend. Some pretty bad vocals from Het.  This is the first track that I might actually like if it was an instrumental.  Musically, it builds up nicely to a fairly heavy ending.  Vocals from both singers ruin the song.<br /><br />Frustration--Reed's vocals border on ludicrous on this track; it feels more like a joke than music.  Anxiety-filled ambient noise goes into a Sabbath-inspired doomy riff that doesn't sound too bad, but Lou sure does.  Again, if this was an instrumental, it would be much more tolerable; not great, but tolerable.  It rocks out admirably near the end of the song, but there's almost a straight rip-off of the riff from C.O.C.'s Paranoid Opioid at 7:14 and 7:48.<br /><br />Little Dog--At this point, after seven songs and about 48 minutes, it's all starting to sound the same.  Some low-key acoustic playing, ambient background noise, Lou talking/rambling, sometimes trying to sing a few notes seemingly at random, a somewhat heavy hard rock riff.  It gets a bit redundant this far in, and there's still over half an hour left after this song.  I'm ready to do something else now, which is not a good sign when I'm listening to a new album.<br /><br />Dragon--Beginning doesn't sound any different from the last song.  Now it's getting extremely redundant...what seemed random during the first half now seems formulaic...some noise, throw in a riff, back to some noise.  Lou talks, tries to sing, goes back to talking.  Mighty boring.  Once the riff kicks in, it sounds like a Load throwaway.  There's a heavier riff in the second half of the song, but it sounds tired.<br /><br />Junior Dad--This one could sum up the whole album; 19 long minutes of the same formula; acoustic, noise, riff, repeat.  With almost twenty minutes to fill, though, Junior Dad goes long on the noise, with minutes-long sections of droning guitar sounds.  It's not that it sounds particularly bad, it just gets boring after so much repetition.  Maybe if an instrumental version comes out, I'll give it another chance, but for now I don't expect to be listening to Lulu again anytime soon.<br /><br />I know these guys are Rock n' Roll Hall of Famers and all, but both artists involved have done far better in their careers.  I have no problem with them doing whatever they want to do.  It's their right and they've earned it.  However, this is a review of the music on the album, not the artists' careers, and the music is not very interesting from a metal/ hard rock fan's perspective.  There are rare moments (and I do mean rare) when Lou's vocal style actually complements the music, and for a few seconds it works.  But those moments are fleeting, and the rest of the time it just doesn't even sound serious.  I don't blame Metallica; I'm sure if Mick Jagger asked them to play music for him on an album, they'd do it in a heartbeat, just to say they played all of the music on an album from a rock legend.  Unfortunately, an honest assessment of the overall musical quality of Lulu doesn't yield high marks.

1423389387|5.0|The Wise Man's Fear picks up where The Name of the Wind left off and continues the story of the life of Kvothe; university student, musician, aspiring arcanist, and much, much more. In truth, it is not so much a novel as a compilation of several distinct adventures. Because of this, I never found myself pondering how the book might end. I was more likely to wonder what was going to happen next.<br /><br />While many are tempted to call Patrick Rothfuss' Kingkiller Chronicle a trilogy, the word doesn't really do it justice. This is unlike most trilogies, where each book is part of the larger story yet still makes an attempt to present a semi-self contained story of its own. Rothfuss himself acknowledges this when he calls this a chronicle. He doesn't even bother to make it plural. This is considered by the author, as it should be by the reader, one chronicle of the adventures of Kvothe.<br /><br />This review is based on the Brilliance Audio production of The Wise Man's Fear. As the Kingkiller Chronicle is essentially the first-person telling of Kvothe's adventures, listening to a quality audio version is an excellent way to experience Rothfuss' books. Nick Podehl has a great voice for reading The Wise Man's Fear. He has a talent for changing his accent or the timber of his voice to present a wide variety of different characters' personalities. My only complaint, and it is minor, is that Podehl could stand to check the pronunciation of some words that are not in common usage in the modern world. While his pronouncing gibbet with a soft 'g', viscount with an 'iss' sound, ha'penny with a short 'a' and two pence as 'too-pence' doesn't ruin the story for me, it does cause me to occasionally roll my eyes and sigh.<br /><br />Young  readers?<br />I would have no problem reading this book with my teenage daughters but it does contain some mild adult language, conversations that scenes that, while not explicit, do not try hard to conceal what is happening. Keep in mind that Kvothe is now at an age where most men spend a good deal of time thinking about, in the author's words, cavorting. Parents concerned about such things may wish to pre-screen these books.<br /><br />The review copy of this book was obtained from the publisher via the Amazon Vine Program.ost men spend a good deal of time thinking about, in the author's words, cavorting.
